CRICKET MATCH AT HAZEL GROVE.—A cricket match was played between the Hazel Grove C.C. and the Bredbury Freebooters. The following were the result: Hazel Grove C.C.: J. Wood 5 F. Main 20, H. Basile 5 Ellsworth 5, F. Hallworth b. Hebel 2, A. Entwisle b. Hantz 2, Roger Mure 0 (captain) b. F. Basils 0, J. Armitstead b. F. Mure 0, R. Horner 6, F. Springett b. H. Hallworth b. R. Roberts 0, G. Bright 18, extras 5; total 61. Hazel Grove Freebooters: T. Moore b. Hare 0, J. Roberts b. R. Moore 39, A. Hawwood c P. Spilsbury b. Harte 8, De Braewa b. Jasne 2, J. Delworth b. Hallward 8, Home 2, W. Oldham lbw b J. F. Wood 14, F. Pantis b R. Moore 1, J. Gunton b. Wood 2, T. W’rithy b. Daniel 11, H. Bickett 25, J. Needham 9, extras 7; total 119.

Stockport v. Little Lever.
This match was played at Little Lever in splendid weather and before a large attendance of spectators. A start was not made till quite three o’clock owing to the Stockport men being delayed on the road. The match was set apart for the benefit of Penry, one of the Little Lever professionals, and one who has done splendid service with the ball, this year; his five years with the club, Stockport still deplored the services of Fred Torkington and one or two others, while Little Lever had their strongest team in the field, this season. Little Lever won the toss, and electing to bat, soon saw Towler and Tylsdesley, to the bowling of Brown and F. Tyler. Tylsdesley had a splendid reception on coming out—a fact due, no doubt, to that player being admitted to the Lancashire County ranks, and it will be remembered that on his second appearance for his county he scored a brave half century at Lord’s against Warwickshire. Brown opened to Towler, sending him first ball, and the ex-tail bowler had him leg stump. A Smith came next, but after scoring six he skied a lavishly-flown “pool his wicket-keeper, joined Tylsdesley, but unfortunately after Tylsdesley was splendidly taken by Bates right on the boundary, and three good wicket were down for seven. Pool came next, and soon began to force matters, driving Brown out of the ground for four. We may say the ground is a very small one inside, it being so much used by cattle. Such scores could not long survive. The score rose steadily, and a chance of Towler was tried at 30. Harry May displaying Tyler; the change almost worked immediately. May bowling Pool for a well hit 20. Hallows came next, and got May away to leg nicely for three, but at the other end Brown bowled him with a grand ball, and had the wicket more down for 45 runs. Sims joined Smith, the latter with his score at 16 was missed by Bates at long on. Sims was then next to go, Brown spotting his wicket 51—5. H. Houlton followed, and hit up 11 in four hits, but was then beautifully taken by Harold Hartley off Brown. 65—7. Norbury came next, and bore a little hand was made, Northrop getting May away for three while Smith got the same bowler for a two under. The score had reached 81 when Brown disturbed Smith’s wicket for a lucky innings of 32. A strong coincidence took place during Smith’s innings, and one which certainly did not reflect much credit on the Little Lever umpire. W. Hartley, who had displayed May, was bowling his last ball of an over, Smith hit the ball out to Bates at square leg and was caught, the umpire, in the meantime, saying “over,” and when Bates had caught Smith the umpire gave it a “no ball,” and thus Smith was allowed to proceed with his innings. The old soon came, the innings closing for 91. Sam Brown deserves great credit for his splendid bowling, taking no fewer than eight wickets for 37 runs, hitting the stumps six times, while the other two were caught. Stockport opened their venture in a dreadfully bad light. J. Bates and W. Marley being the first batsmen to face Kutson and Penry. Hatson first Torkington joined the captain, but after scoring five shared the fate of Bates. Brown came next, and hitting one out to Tylsdesley was splendidly caught and three wickets were down for 11 runs. Rain then commenced to fall, and put an end to all further play, and what would have been an interesting finish was spoiled. The Stockport team fielded again up to form. On Saturday next Werneth are down at Gtld Green, and the match is set apart for the benefit of Sam Brown and Jim Disney. As this is one of the best fixtures on the card and a fixture which those well with the Stockport people, the two “pros.” should materially benefit by it. Given a fine day a grand game is expected. Stockport v. Little Lever.
This match was played at Little Lever in splendid weather and before a large attendance of spectators. A start was not made till quite three o'clock owing to the Stockport men being delayed on the road. The match was set apart for the benefit of Penny, one of the Little Lever professionals, and one who has done splendid service with the ball, this being his first year with them. Stockport still deplored the services of Fred Torkington and one or two others, while Little Lever had their strongest team in the field this season. Little Lever won the toss, and electing to bat sent in Towler and Tyldesley to the bowling of Brown and F. Tyler. Tyldesley had a splendid reception on coming out—a fact due, no doubt, to that player being admitted to the Lancashire County ranks, and it will be remembered that on his second appearance for his county he scored no fewer than 158 not out against Warwickshire. Brown opened to Towler, beating him first ball, and the next ball bowled him leg before. A Smith came next, but after scoring six in wretched fashion Brown upset his wicket. C. Smith, the Lancashire County wicket-keeper, joined Tyldesley, but immediately after Tyldesley was splendidly taken by Bates right on the boundary, and three good wickets were down for seven. Pooi came next, and soon began to force matters, hitting Brown out of the ground for four. We may say the ground is a very small one indeed, it being no trouble to get a ball out. Smith seemed content with singles. The score rose steadily, and a change of bowling was tried at 30. Harry May displaced Tyler; the change almost worked immediately. May bowling Pooi for a well hit Hallows came next, and got his way to leg nicely for three, but at the other end Brown bowled him with a grand ball, and half the wickets were down for 45 runs. Simm joined Smith; the latter with his score at 16 was missed by Bates at long on. Simm was the next to go, Brown upsetting his wicket. 51—6-0. Knutton followed, and hit up 11 in fine style, but was then beautifully taken by Harold Hartley off Brown. 65—7—11. Northrop came next, and here a little stand was made. Northrop getting May away for three while Smith at the same bowler for a like number. The score had reached 81 when Brown disturbed Smith's wicket for a lucky innings of 32. A strange coincidence took place during Smith's innings, and one which certainly did not reflect much credit on the Little Lever umpire. W. Hartley, who had displaced May, was bowling his last ball of his second over, Smith hit the ball out to Bates at square leg and was caught, the umpire, in the meantime, saying “4 over,” and when Bates had caught Smith the umpire gave it “No ball,” and this Smith was allowed to proceed with his innings. The end soon came, the innings closing for 91. Sam Brown deserves great credit for his splendid bowling, taking no fewer than eight wickets for 37 runs, hitting the stumps six times, while the other two were caught. Stockport opened her venture in a dreadful bad light. J. Bates and W. Hartley being the first batsmen to the Lancashire and Derby. Bates after scoring a single was caught at the wicket. A. Torkington joined his captain, but after scoring five shared the fate of Bates. Brown came next, and hitting one out to Tyldesley was splendidly caught, and three wickets were down for 11 runs. Rain then commenced to fall, and put an end to all further play, and what would have been an interesting finish was spoiled. The Stockport team fielded quite up to form. On Saturday next Westhaugh are down at Cale Green, and this match is set apart for the benefit of Sam Brown and Jimmy Diney. As this is one of the best fixtures on the card and a fixture which takes up with the Stockport people, the two “pros.” should materially benefit by it. Given a fine day, a grand game is expected. So roll up and assist your old friends. Below are appended the scores and analysis:—

CRICKET MATCH AT HAZEL GROVE.
A cricket match was played on the Hazel Grove Cricket Ground at Norbury on Monday afternoon, between Hazel Grove and Poynton. For several years past these two rival clubs have contested for supremacy, and considerable interest was taken in the match. In consequence of the annual Wakes a considerable number of people were enabled to be present and evinced a lively interest in the proceedings. The Hazel Grove Brass Band was in attendance and played lively selections of music during the afternoon. During the earlier part of the afternoon the rain fell in torrents, and it was thought that the match would have to be abandoned. At three o'clock, however, the time fixed for the commencement of the match, the rain cleared and was succeeded by brilliant sunshine. Both elevens were fairly representative of the two clubs. Poynton, however, had a strong team, while Hazel Grove were short of the services of Gledhill, Penney, and Wood. The home captain won the toss and elected to bat. Cleaver and Fallow were the first to wield the willow, to the bowling of Rhodes and Clarke. Play was for a time rather dull, runs coming very slowly. Cleaver, the first man in, was neatly caught when he had succeeded in knocking up 8. Fallow's stay was very short, a solitary one falling to his lot. Standring's career was cut short before he managed to break the duck. Roberts and Penney played well together, the latter especially batting steadily. Roberts, although he played a cautious game, was only successful in obtaining four runs, Wadsworth, with a neat catch, completely put a stop to his batting. Only about 30 runs had been scored at four o'clock, while five wickets had fallen, and the prospect for the home eleven began to look rather gloomy. Penney, who was playing a good game, was now partnered by Daniels, who lost no time in breaking his duck, and by quiet efforts these players speedily succeeded in raising the score marked on the indicator to 40. Daniels sent a beauty to leg for two, which was speedily followed by a three. His good luck, however, now came to an end, and the next ball took his stick. The rest of the Hazel Grove team were quickly disposed of. Smith and Clayton were the first to take their stand against the bowling of Standring and Bennett. It was soon evident to the spectators that Poynton's chances of winning were very indeed. The Poynton lads batted well, and notwithstanding the good fielding of the homesters, quickly increased the score.
Hazel Grove 53, Poynton 33.
The top score for Poynton was 25, made by A. Clayton. W. Bailey came second with 24 to his credit.

CHESHIRE COUNTY NEWS: FRIDAY 23 AUGUST 1895-2.

CRICKET.
SATURDAY'S MATCHES.
Stockport v. Werneth.
STOCKPORT WIN BRILLIANTLY.
This match between these old rivals was played at Cale Green Park in magnificent weather, and before a large crowd of spectators, it being also set apart for the benefit of the professional, Brown and Dixon, who throughout the season have done splendid work, and it is hoped that they will receive a substantial reward. It will be remembered that the first time these teams met at Werneth this season Stockport won a most interesting match by eleven runs, due on the whole to the splendid bowling of the evergreen Sam Brown. On Saturday skipper Bartley won the toss, and of course chose to bat, so as to have wickets likely in with him. Bates to face the bowling of Hall and Hodgkinson. The first was soon too good from a Stockport point of view, as Bates in trying to cut a ball from Hodgkinson to leg was elegantly caught at slip. Arthur Torrington joined his captain, and at Hall splendidly for four, but after scoring ten he was caught at the wicket. Sam Brown came next and was warmly greeted by the crowd. He immediately got to work, scoring many lusty hits. Two overs had reached 37 when Erickson was caught by Riddell, after that player had scored 32. Harry Jones in front professional not only to see his brilliantly carried it to 56, but Colliery, after perhaps a splendid game. H. A. Bromley-Davenport of Cheshire county and Cambridge University fame came next. At three a splendid stand took place, both men doing all the offence of the Werneth bowlers. Davenport delighted the spectators with his big hitting, when Dewey, one like other hand, was playing a most finished game, his cutting being a treat. At 72 Davenport was run out in jumping forward to Hall's ball from Bacon, who hit the wicket keeper's pads and was thereby able to use which note. Davenport could never have improved, he had hit up 17 in a very few minutes and he was loudly cheered on retiring. F. Tylers joined Dixon, but shortly after the latter was caught at mid off for a splendidly played innings of 21. The telegraph board now reading 87-5-21. F. Tyler, however, put in the lead for further commitment with the exception of Frank Tyler's display, which was a rarity particularly to sportsmen. He swung 34 not out in clock and won two three's, and three two's. He was then assisted by J. B. Taylor, who played a ten minute not always before being caught on the boundary. The innings closed for the respectable score of 139. After the opening interval Werneth opened their venture by sending in Warmby and Dixon, the stock being turned to W. T. Brown and Hall, thereby Davenport and Brown quitted with a mixed order, but Eaton for the mantles at first with Hall always for the bat and one over given by both over point, the first wicket falling at twelve. Early on top Warmby played his own magnificent and excellent Hall, that player being bowled. He had twice used from Brown. Redfern was next, his left hand ball proving too difficult. The whole men, and the wicket were down for 15 runs. Hall too played one over with the exception of 10 from Hall, and score, no one seemed able to play the bowling, the innings closing for 16. Hopkins the local crack acted like a won over Werneth for the second time this season. I must just make a note of the splendid bowling of Sam Brown and Harry May, the former taking 12 for 18, and the latter 1? for 15 and this once with the splendid fielding of the Stockport men, who never missed a chance, was due in no small measure to their brilliant victory. The spectators seemed to fairly enjoy the afternoon's sport, and especially so to see their own men turn up to the right side.

BIRKENHEAD ADVERTISER: SATURDAY 24 AUGUST 1895 - 1.

CRICKET.
BY UBIQUE.

Respected Sir,—Far be it from me to deny, or even deprecate, the gingerbread-making qualities of that marvellous place, Ormskirk; but, sir, I must gently but firmly draw the line at what the Ormskirk natives glorify by the name of cricket. Cricket, forsooth! I—Eh! Where's Ormskirk? You don't mean to say you never heard of Peerless Ormskirk? Dear, dear! What appalling ignorance! Why, you can find it on the map with a microscope and a week to spare—

Oh! Ormskirk is a marvellous spot;
A marvellous spot it be.
And woe to the man who says it's not:
(With bullets of gingerbread he'll be shot)—
For the native can point on the map to a blot
As big as the eye of a flea!

And it boasts a standing population of a cricket (?) team, seven enthusiastic spectators, and a small dog. Oh! and also a gent. what scores for the team and makes speeches with an average of 45 perorations about the paralysing miracles of batting with which the players weekly astound the world as it stares gaping and transfixed with wonder and awe. What's that? Well, if you don't believe me ask the select audience which was grouped within and around the scoring box last Saturday, and which went black in the face with suppressed laughter as the Petrifying Puffer held forth during the progress of the fiasco between Ormsquirk and the Park.

Yes, sir! Fiasco is the right and only term to apply to the occasion,—none other genuine. For I do not speak without serious consideration when I protest that of all they and various samples of the noble art of batting I have seen, never—I emphatically repeat—never have I experienced so insufferably feeble and unworthy an exhibition as that of Ormskirk on the Park's ground last week-end. Faugh! I scarce have patience to think of it; —of the hundreds at the match not one spectator with a spark of manliness or sportsmanship in him, could look at the farce without a sneer of contempt or a disgusted exclamation. And the remarks of the scornful crowd were numerous and varied, but very much to the point. But let me rouse your indignation by a short description of the thing. In the first place, to show there was no ill-feeling, the Park lost the toss; which, considering Ormskirk's somnolent methods, was fatal. For when Ormskirk goes to the wickets and begins to “bat” the spectators are liable to fall into a refreshing sleep. Though, to speak fairly, and give them their due, I have known an Ormskirk player, under favourable circumstances, to make a two hit! But one cannot always expect such a feat.

Well, the gingerbread purveyor, with great assiduity commenced to block every ball that toddled up the pitch and the scoring for an hour averaged upwards of one run to each over! But what price this,—Ormskirk took an hour to score their first 30 runs! We had to pour cold water down the Park scorer's neck to keep him awake,—he wasn't used to it. All sorts of bowlers were tried, but it was block, block, block, and nothing came of it, but frantic yells of delight from the crowd when one of the stone-wallers scored a single! Which was a rare occurrence. As time dragged wearily on, and maiden overs were painfully fashionable, I could stand it no longer, and went for a walk to have a quiet swear to myself. When I returned—joy! the last Ormskirk stick in the mud was bowled, and I then discovered that the whole eleven had positively and actually taken up 3 (three!!) hours in scoring a total of 149 runs! I muttered a prayer of thanksgiving that it was all over, and washed it down with a No. 9 Pilkin.

You may bet your respectability that Holden and Co. were just itching to lay their hands on the bat, and show these timid Ormskirkers how runs were made. And, by the Hokey!—but stay; all in good time. As the gingerbread gents had very carefully contrived to leave the Park's headquarters of an hour in which to bat, our premieres could not expect to give their opponents the all-powerful thrashing they administered at the last meeting. So Ormskirk had gained their end. Well, Holden and Ould opened the cannonade, and I may say at once that the captain's phenomenal display of gigantic hitting has rarely, if ever, been equalled on the Park ground,—which is a large order! Bennion and Barnes were the trundlers to suffer. I kept for them. Thirty-four runs in five overs! Four upon four came from Cecil's wonderful strokes, and at one time he followed up four successive boundary hits by swiping the ball clean over the pavilion for 6! What a paralysing contrast to Ormskirk's senile pottering at the crease! For, though but 30 minutes at the wickets Holden piled up 51 runs, which, with the exception of five singles, was one mass of 4's with a 6 thrown in for the sake of variety. With apologies to “The Man that Struck O'Hara,”—

First he spanked 'em through the crowd,
Then he banged 'em where he chose;
Then he had another go—
Smiting sixes fours and twos!
Such a sight you never saw—
(Ormskirk but for time would lose)
Rat-tat-tat!
A whirling bat,
A high-skied ball
Was left—that was all—
(Of poor Ormskirk's grovelled bowling.

And the sanguine scorer who made speeches said “Well, I never did!” and tried to change the subject. When time arrived the Park had totalled 75 in three quarters of an hour, and having six wickets in hand, nothing came of it—to the visitors' relief. Of course, had there been time to win the home eleven would have adopted steadier tactics, but Cecil always knows when to do the right thing, more power to him. And in awakening the crowd sleeping under the influence of the Ormskirk-narcotic, he did the greatest thing of all. Ormskirk! Bah! I'd strike 'em off the fixture list, eh W—?

When Oxton tried conclusions with Huyton, at Oxton, on Saturday, a Liverpool criticaster remarks, “small scoring was the order.” Of course, it's purely a matter of taste, but though “small scoring” was the order for Oxton and Huyton, some degraded people would prefer their order to be small Scotch! Not too small, but just lemon enough. [The Local Vetoist will please excuse this culpable burst of Humanity.] Huyton, batting first, fell away, so to speak, on the spot. Their experience was, indeed, a sad one, for the Bowling Bishop and Smith relieved them of their duties by the time the total had bobbed up by jerky stages to 55. Oxton smiled largely as they proceeded to knock 'em off. But Oxton are sanguine persons, and you never can tell. For our suburbanas manifested an alarming propensity for hatching “ducks,” and wanted—very badly—about 10 runs to win when the ninth wicket went by the board. But—dear old “but”!—amid tremendous excitement and agitated gaspings, P. Brownell and W. Scott saw the sinking ship safe through the breakers into harbour (pardon the simile), and when the last man was “potted” Oxton had reached the winning post first by 7 runs. Those similes have got a bit mixed up somehow, so we had better escape to Aigburth.

At Aigburth, on Saturday, Rock Ferry were busy replying to Liverpool's challenge. But they were more busy with the ball than the bat, for the castiga-tion they had to submit to covered the whole ground, and so did the South End fielders, many times over. People said that after such a thrashing the hearts of the Rocks would be pained and sore, but all practical persons will agree with me that after such a “burst” of fielding, the soreness would be located in their feet. I merely offer this as an 'umble opinion, and as a guarantee of good faith. However, Far is as what we're hankering after. Rock Ferry opening the batting, immediately printed with the wickets of Kripps, Ravenscroft, and A. Stott, two of whom would get their l.b.w. It was a bad business. This ominous decay was delayed by Needham (32) and T. R. Bretherton (15), but the remainder of the eleven, “not to be denied,” had a little rot “on their own,” which was so successful that the grand total recorded was 67. Not very dazzling, was it? By the way, Needham was also out l.b.w. Three leg-before decisions are enough to drive an eleven into an asylum—or—hotel. Minus the discomfort of having to face E. C. Ryland—who would have done them a world of good on such a wicket—Liverpool enjoyed themselves, as I have hinted, and the fun waxed fast and—faster (Ha, ha! caught you tripping there, eh?) and when hostilities ceased they had piled up 174 for 7 wickets. The only South End bowler to make an impression was A. Stott, who captured 6 of the 7 wickets for only 39 runs, if Ryland had been there to help him, who can tell what—but “ifs” are idle now.

As I witnessed part of the contest between Victoria and New Brighton, at the Park, on Saturday, I certainly expected the Vics to annex what I thought would be a well-merited triumph. Instead of which they were beaten by 31 runs, so that New Brighton have avenged the unhappy downfall they underwent when the season was young. A trifle more of that estimable cricketing virtue, consistency, would have given the homesters the palm, for they could unfortunately only produce three double-figures, viz.:—J. M'Donald—the substantial old warrior—(45), J. Wylde (23), and G. Lumgair (16). Only this and nothing more. And I would respectfully inquire of such hitters as W. T., J. Brocklebank, Tom Fish, &c., why this unwarrantable huskiness? Now, on the other hand, the visitors' distribution of duty gives one a sensation of satisfaction when looking over their list, as the double-figurists were several, and strong. Yes, “we are seven.”

At last! our Police 1st eleven have had to knuckle under and sing exceedingly small; though Brombro' Pool on Saturday surely “made them dance.” The contest, which was witnessed at the Pool, was a surprise and no mistake. In this way. The home eleven—who are famously erratic in mixing up,—

Great deeds and doings small,
Other deeds and none at all!

with the assistance of J. Pugh (44 not out), and the Rev. F. B. (20), toted up 123. The boys in “white” promised to cover this in double-quick time, A. Wright and J. Howard—each 20 runs—commencing the visitors' retaliation in brisk fashion. But the ways of the Brombro Bowlers are curious and inscru-table, and lo and behold! likewise ecco! not another policeman could stand before them; the remaining 9 routed to a man, and the constables' feelings were hurt when they suddenly discovered that they were beaten by 33 runs!

A nameless individual writes:—
Dear Sir,—Referring to the remarks in your cricket notes on Saturday last re the ticket match Bromboro' Pool v. Birkenhead Victoria, I notice, as on many previous occasions, you do not give the Poolers the credit that is due to them. You state that the Pool had first run at the wickets, which was a considerable advantage. Now this is not the case, as the Victoria had the first run of the wicket, and being that you attribute their downfall partly to them having to bat second, it makes the Pool's victory all the more meritorious. Hoping for the future you will be a little more correct in your reports.
Interested.
The italics are mine. I am not surprised that “Interested” had not sufficient manliness to enclose his name and address; as it is quite in the nature of things that a person who can stoop to such untruthful allegations, can only be expected to strike in the dark. As to my last Saturday' mistake, as Brombro' Pool never trouble to send me the reports of their engagements, I must obtain information from whatever other source is available; and if an error thus creeps in, the Pool have only themselves to thank. As to the anonymous snarl that on “many previous occasions” I have not given the Pool due credit, if my hidden gentleman will step out into the light like an honest man, drop generalities for facts, and name his “occasions,” I shall be able to deal with him as he deserves.

As I was not able to see the Park v. Victoria engagement on Wednesday, I insert the following remarks of a correspondent:—“The Park, batting first was a queer team, made 110. In reply the Vics. had made 60 for 3 wickets when Holden went on to bowl. He was also knocked about for a time, but, changing ends, he put 'em in with terrible effect, and upset the last 6 wickets for 8 runs!” The Vics totalled 81. Comment would be useless.

New Brighton's meeting with Liverpool on Wednesday proved disastrous, for in reply to the Liverpudlians 239 they could only notch 58!

Cheshire went down again this week, being beaten by Worcester to the tune of 7 wickets, though the scoring all round was low.

On Saturday, Mr. Wynne's team thrashed Mr. Griffith's team by 84 to 47, the top-scorer being F. Nicholls (20) for the “Wynners'!” Scores only received yesterday.—Six days after the event—much too late for insertion.

Cricket in Wirral on Saturday, was plentifully bestrewn with insignificant contributions. For instance Neston and District had a bout with Kirkdale Wesleyans, and thrashed them by 99 to 46. Dr. Speechless—beg pardon, Speechly (22) being the shining star.—Again at Port Sunlight—the Cheshire Sweet Auburn—the Sophes had totalled 55 and placed four Helsby champions hors de combat for 30 runs when the game terminated.

Spital, on their own ground on Saturday, did a big thing, for Boughton Hall were just saved from a smart whipping at their instigation. The home exponents gave a tasty display of solid and consistent batting, which ended in a rather large 193 for only 8 wickets; and they had followed this up by clearing off six of the Hall gents. for a pigmy 77 when time interfered, and the contest ended in what some people—at Chester—call a draw. Hardly!

On Monday and Tuesday last Woodland played their last match, when they were opposed by the Kingsley, whom they whacked by 5 wickets and 9 runs. Woodland throughout the season have:—
Played Won. Lost. Drawn.
29 22 7 none.
Which I think is very good for a junior club.

A correspondent sayeth:—‘The return match between St. Mary's and Tranmere Wesley was played at Woodchurch-road on Saturday, the home team being somewhat late in arriving. The Saints—as distinct from the “Wesleyans”!—batted first, two wickets at once falling for 3 runs! Matters, however, soon changed, Linekar, Sault, Gaskell, F. Williams and S. Jenkins getting well into double figures and completely mastering the bowling. Gaskell in particular hitting with characteristic vigour. With one man short, the total reached 92. Tranmere Wesley started well, T. Francis and Buckley getting into double figures, but the remainder gave little inconvenience, and time alone saved them—due to the late start—from defeat, 8 wickets having realised only 62 runs.’ Oh, Time, Time! you have a heap to answer for!—The proceedings of the second

BIRKENHEAD VICTORIA v. NEW BRIGHTON.—At
Birkenhead Park. Scores :—

MACCLESFIELD CHRONICLE: FRIDAY 30 AUGUST 1895.-2.

Stockport v. Cheadle Hulme.
Great interest was manifested in the meeting of those local rivals, which took place on the Cheadle Hulme ground in splendid weather and before a good attendance of spectators on Saturday afternoon. It was also of apart as the benefit of the Cheadle Hulme professional, Teather. The first match between these elevens this season ended in a very even draw. Shipperl Ralston lost the toss, and of course had to field. C. V. Rome and Teather were the first batsmen to open the Cheadle innings to the bowling of Sam Brown and H. May. Teather scored a single off Brown, but next over Harry May knocked his leg stump out of the ground. E Rome came next, and immediately cut Brown for four, while O Rome got May away for a like number, but was out soon after, Brown taking him well at short slip. 12—2-5. Briggs joined E. Rome, but with an addition of eight runs one Rome was finely caught by Hartley after scoring 10. A. Royle was sent in, and saw Briggs go. Brown away to leg for four, and next over repeated the stroke very luckily. Royle hit May for three, but soon after had a very bad missfield—caught by Harry May at short slip bowled—a very fine catch indeed. C. Taylor joined Briggs, and here a stand took place, though Briggs did most scoring. At 62, however, Fred Torkington, who had displaced

May, clean bowled Briggs, that player having made 27 in not a very taking fashion; the ball the wicket were down for 62. W. Redfern came next, but without addition Fred Torkington upset his middle stump with one of his expresses. Taylor left at 67 and Rome at 69, while Kirk left at 71, there being nine wickets down. Then came a surprise for the Stockport men. Todd and Collen got getting another made things very lively for the fielders. Chases of bowling were tried, but to no use, the 100 being up amidst loud applause; both men played a fair game, Todd several times getting the ball away in nice style. W. Hartley went on with lobs, but eased on grass season. When the total had reached 127 Colles was bowled by Fred Torkington, his innings of 15, coming at a time when things were looking serious, was of the utmost value to his side, while too much cannot be said of Todd, who carried out his bat for a splendid innings of 34. Certainly the Stockport men assisted them materially by their wretched fielding, but nevertheless they deserve great credit for their plucky partnership. Fred Torkington came out with a splendid analysis out of 132 overs; he bowled six maidens for eight runs and took three wickets. May had three for 25, and Brown three for 33. At ten minutes past five W. Hartley and J. Bates came out on behalf of Stockport, the bowling being entrusted to Teather and Redfern. Bates cut Teather finely for four, while Hartley off-drove Redfern soon after for a similar number. Rome came all good pace. With the score at 24 Bates was bowled by a ball from Teather which came several inches across, and removed his leg bail; that player had scored fourteen in quick time. Arthur Torkington joined his captain, only to see him given out caught at the wicket, the decision which seemed doubtful. Brown came next, and here some of the finest cricket of the day was seen both men hit out well. Still the bowling was good but runs coming at a fair pace. Briggs displaced Teather when the score had reached 72. Torkington was bowled for a splendid innings of 24, which included three fours and four twos. E. Tyler came next, but after scoring three was bowled. H. Hartley came next, and the first ball he received he drove it magnificently right out of the ground for four. This stroke quite taking the bowler by surprise. Sam Brown was not soon after from a big hit, which E. Rome took very well, after playing a grand game for 27, his innings included the fours and two twos. Disney came next and saw Hartley lay about both bowlers. Briggs coming in for a few short of punishment, he getting that player away for two fours, but in attempting to repeat was caught at the wicket; his brilliant innings of 15 included three fours, one three, one two, and a single. He tried his very utmost to bring the match, and indeed could he have stayed another quarter of an hour I believe his object would have been accomplished. Disney left at the same total, 103. With May and Selby in time was called, no further wicket being down for 110; thus the match ended in a draw in favour of the visitors. Below is appended full scores and analysis:—

MIDDLESEX v. LANCASHIRE.
St. Joseph's met Hyde St. Thomas' in their last league match at Reddish on Saturday last, winning by 50 against 23, thus for the second time proving the champions of the second division of the North-East Cheshire League. They have played 12 league matches, lost 0, won 9, drawn 3. LIVERPOOL POST: FRIDAY 13 SEPTEMBER 1895: TUESDAY 17 Sep 1895

LIVERPOOL CRICKET CLUB.
BATTING AND BOWLING AVERAGES.
The first eleven played 37 matches, won 16 lost 9, and 12 were drawn. The first eleven played 17 matches, won 8, lost 1, and drew 8, scoring 3,873 runs (including 311 extras) in the season of 294 wickets, against 3,897 runs (including 352 extras) for the loss of 261 wickets. The club eleven played 6 matches, of which 1 was won and 2 drawn. In all the club played 80 matches, of which 33 were won, 17 lost, and 30 drawn. District matches were played against Yorkshire, County and Cambridge University, the former being won and the latter lost. In addition, 2 extra matches were played, giving a total of 97 matches during the season. Although Mr. A. F. Kemble has only taken part in 21 matches this season, he has surpassed in stumpings and catching 41 opponents.

ST. AIDAN'S.
The past season has been a most successful one for
this club. Of 19 matches played the team has won
17 losing two only, and one of those by the narrow
majority of 7 runs. The total number of runs they
are credited with is 1,497 for 179 wickets, against
593 for 190 wickets for opponents. The highest score
obtained was 133 against Brunswick Y.M.C. Guild
Six times during the season was the century over-
topped by the Aidaniens, whilst the highest score
made against them was 88. G. Lewis’s 70 (not out)
was the highest individual score for the Saints during
the season.

The First Eleven this year have done a little better than
last. The averages show a big improvement.

A match between Liverpool and Oxton culminated
in a win for Liverpool by 62 runs and 3 wickets.
For Oxton, M. Field, H. S. Hibbert, and J. Curwen
were the only double figures in a score of 71.

Rock Ferry administered a severe thrashing to
Cheadle Hulme. The Ferry batted first, and after
scoring 126 for 9 wickets closed their innings, and
their opponents, in reply, could only muster 32. The
chief scorers for Rock Ferry were J. Ravenscroft
(43) and C. Stobart (23).

New Brighton were once more obliged to knuckle
under on Saturday, their conquerors this time being
Formby. New Brighton, batting first, managed to
amass 89 runs, chiefly through the aid of Moore (27),
E. Drinkwater (11), and F. Pepper (10), these being
the only New Brightonians who succeeded in reach-
ing double figures. Formby showed too much better
advantage, as they managed to compile 108 for 7
wickets.

Wallasey took on the second string of Oxton, on
the latter’s ground, and succeeded in defeating them
by 20 runs, J. C. Kay (29) and G. Turner (18)
being chiefly instrumental in securing Oxton’s down-
fall.

Bootle secured an easy victory over Birkenhead
Victoria, the scene of the conflict being Bootle.
The Victorians batted first and scored 71, of which F.
Wright compiled 22 (not out), Bootle replying with
126 for 6 wickets.

Another easy victory was that of Bromboro’ over
Spital, whom they swamped to the tune of 61 with 4
wickets to spare. For Bromboro’ the chief scorers were
found in J. Pugh with 50, and H. Pugh with 36,
while for the losers W. Bowring and G. Earl were first
with 22 and 11 respectively.

Tranmere Wesley overcame West Derby 2nd by
sixteen runs, J. Turnbull (12) being the only double
figurist in that match. Buckley and Quinn in the
bowling department took 4 wickets each at a cost of
thirteen runs.

Y.M.C.A. finished a generally successful season by
defeating Ravenscroft by the substantial majority of
61, E. R. Davies, R. Willcey, and L. Bunting show-
ing good form with the bat for scores of 26, 12, and
11 respectively.

FIXTURES FOR TO-DAY.
Oxton A v Otterspool, away.
Oxton Extra v Rev. G. D. White’s eleven, at Oxton.
Mersey 2nd v Liverpool Overhead, away.
Birkenhead St. Mary’s v Newsham A, at Newsham
Park; 2nd eleven v Grosvenor, at Birkenhead
Park.
St. John’s A v Seacombe St. Paul’s, away.
Tranmere Wesley v Neston, at Neston.
Bromborough Pool v Walton, at Walton ; 2nd elevens,
at the Pool.

BIRKENHEAD NEWS: SATURDAY 14 SEPTEMBER 1895-2.

CRICKET NOTES.

Saturday saw the practical conclusion of the season, the leading local clubs all finishing their list of engagements. The Premier club should have played Huyton, but as the latter's ground was nothing short of a quagmire after the heavy rain of the previous evening, the match had perforce to be abandoned. It is some time back now since the Parkites have had such a successful season as the one just concluded, as their position at the head of the district competition readily testified. Independent, however, of their excellent record against the district clubs, they have done more than well against such powerful opponents as those of Manchester, Leyland and Wigan, the latter being beaten in both their engagements with the Park club. It will be seen on reference to the batting and bowling averages that T. R. Hubback heads the list in both departments of the game, a position which indicates what an invaluable acquisition he has been to his club with both bat and ball, independent of the great service he has rendered behind the wicket. R. Fielding could has secured the second position in the batting list, being closely followed by H. J. Fish, who, owing to a severe illness has been prevented from taking part in several important matches. On the whole skipper Holden can hardly be said to have played up to the form exhibited by him for several previous years, and it looks a trifle curious to see his name so low down in the list as fourth, but still an average of 31.11 for 26 innings is by no means bad, and besides this he has rendered excellent service with the ball, having captured 45 wickets at an average cost of 9.20. Indeed he has seldom bowled better than he has done this season, and he is to be congratulated on his capital record with the sphere. H. M. Grayson comes out fairly well with an average of 19.1 for 16 innings, but Smith shows a great falling off as compared with last year, his figures being 16.3 for 19 ventures. Town has done a little better, having an average of 18 for 13 innings, whilst others who figure rather low down, but who may be confidently expected to do much better in the future, include H. W. P. Bennette, H. Salmon, and H. S. Cochrane. That good old stonewaller, A. B. Wilson, does not appear to have exercised his usual discretion, for he comes very near to being the “absolute,” this unenviable position being occupied by Captain Byrne. The brunt of the bowling has of course been borne by Smith, who has taken 88 wickets for 10.9, whilst C. E. Steen's 48 have been secured at a rate of 13.7. Town has had a singularly unfortunate season, as although he has bowled uniformly well he has been able to get but few wickets, 34 at a cost of 22.11 being rather expensive.
The Rock Ferryites concluded a fairly successful season on Saturday with a win over Cheadle Hulme. Of the 25 matches they have played, 11 have been won, 6 drawn, and 7 lost, whilst one had the almost unique result of a tie. A glance at the batting averages shows E. F. Kearsley at the head of affairs with the nice figures of 30.88 for nine innings, and it seems rather a pity that his services are not more often available for first team matches. A. J. Cripps comes next, but his average compares unfavourable with that of Kearsley, there being a considerable difference between 30.88 and 23, albeit Cripps has played two more innings. With the exception of his captain, A. Stott, who is placed third, has taken part in more matches than any other player in the team, and his 21.13 for 24 innings must be set down as distinctly creditable. G. G. Paton has an average of 20.4 for 14 innings, whilst immediately following him comes “Captain” Ravencroft who, on the whole, has been far from fortunate, repeated failures reducing his figures to as low as 19.79 for 26 innings. Another batsman who has not shown to great advantage this season is T. R. Bretherton, whose figures of 18.78 for 20 attempts are not as good as might be expected from such a capable willow wielder, whilst the average of A. C. Blain is also a little disappointing, this usually remarkably vigorous batsman being comparatively low down in the list with 18.33 for 18 innings. Needham's figures are 17.28 for 23 attempts, the next in order of merit being A. C. Bamford, who for 16 innings has an average of 14.46. Surprisingly low down is H. S. Bamford who, however, has not devoted much time to the game, having only been at the wickets on six occasions, for which he has an average of 10.14. J. Bretherton, E. Stott, G. B. Paterson, and E. C. Rylands are also allotted places in the list, the last being Ryland who, out of 14 innings, has been not out no less than seven times, and would probably have had a much higher average than 4.14 if he had been given a chance of showing his abilities with the bat. If he has not proved himself a brilliant batsman, however, there can be no question about his success with the ball, for during the season he has captured no fewer than 113 wickets at the moderate rate of 11.03 per wicket. J. Bretherton, though bowling well, falls far behind Ryland, the fast bowler only crediting himself with 83 wickets at a cost of 12.36. With such strong amateur bowling talent at its disposal, the club was not required to call upon the services of Needham to any great extent, but the pro has been very useful at times, and has secured 26 wickets at an average cost of 17.19.
Though the Oxtonites have been under a cloud for the last two or three weeks, they cannot be said to have fared badly on the whole, as out of 23 matches played they have won 9 and drawn 13, being absolutely beaten on but five occasions, one, the match referred to in my remarks about Rock Ferry, resulting in a tie. On Saturday, at Aigburth, they met with another serious reverse, receiving a shocking trouncing at the hands of the Liverpool representatives, who disposed of them for 71, and knocked up 133 for the loss of seven wickets. The Oxtonites thus ended the season with three successive losses. Their batting averages reveal the fact that M. Court has not only scored the most runs, but he has also the best average, his figures being 32.67 for 27 innings, a really excellent record for this very promising young player. The second position on the list is filled by J. H. F. Grayson, with 27.87 for nine innings, but now that this undoubtedly fine batsman has settled down and definitely thrown in his lot with the Oxtonites, we may expect to see a marked improvement in his figures next year. Another old Park player takes third honours, this being W. Turner, who has developed into one of the mainstays of the Oxton batting, this being evidenced by his excellent average of 25.67 for 25 innings. The Oxton skipper, C. F. Hutton has been a little off colour occasionally during the season, and has not scored quite so prolifically as he has done in former years, his highest contribution in an innings being 52, and his average 20.38 for 15 ventures. That the new pro. Bishop will be a great acquisition to the Oxtonites next season there can be but little doubt, for during the short time he has been engaged he has done wonderfully well with both bat and ball, having a batting average of 19.29 for 9 innings, whilst with the ball he has secured 48 wickets at a cost of 9.4 per wicket. J. Curwen has scored better and more consistently than he has done for a considerable time, and his record of 19.12 for 20 innings indicates that his powers as a batsman have not yet altogether left him. A. H. Tibbert has only taken part in 12 matches, for which his figures are 16.17, whilst for 19 innings M. Field has an average of 14.22. Through an injury received, early on in the season, Smith was unable to take as active a part in the campaign as could be wished, and for his 12 innings he is rather low down on the list with 13.52. H. Bickersteth has played in ten matches, and has scarcely been so successful in taking out his bat as he has been in former seasons, consequently his average has suffered somewhat, and his figures of 10.86 are not calculated to make him unduly-conceited. G. H. Court, B. B. Smith, and P. L. Bromnell are all close together with figures ranging from 10.33 to 8.18, L. Court bringing up the rear with 3.86 for 2 innings. Bowling honours are divided between amateurs and professionals, Bishop coming off well with 41 wickets at a cost of 9.4. Smith has proved a trifle more expensive his figures being 63 for 9.62, but his record is an excellent one for all that, more especially in view of the accident he met with, before referred to. G. H. Court has also rendered splendid service with the ball, capturing no less than 49 wickets, whilst his younger brother L. Court has given signs of future usefulness by securing 12 wickets for 3.86 per wicket.
Like that of Oxton, the Birkenhead Victoria first string have been faring badly of late, and also like Oxton they concluded their campaign on Saturday with a disastrous defeat, the Bootle representatives, who have done anything but well themselves this year, achieving an easy victory over the evidently disorganised Victorians, who made a very feeble show with the bat, and were all sent to the rightabout for 71. On the whole the Victoria representatives can hardly be congratulated on their season's record, for out of 25 matches played they have only won four, whilst 9 have been lost and 12 drawn. It is not surprising to see J. Wylde at the head of the batting averages with 25.14, for he has shown consistent good form throughout the season. Others who may be said to have done well are W. T. Davies (22.14), F. Wright (19.4) W. H. Routledge (18.7) and J. Macdonald (17.3), but a falling off is noticeable in the batting of J. Brocklebank, who is very low down on the list with 14.6, though he has played in more matches than any of his colleagues. J. Fish has been a distinct acquisition with the leather and heads the bowling averages with 27 wickets for a trifle over 11 runs per wicket. So far as taking wickets is concerned, however, J. Brocklebank is an easy first, he having captured 42 at a cost of 14.32. Several others of the team have had a trial with the ball, these including J. West, S. Wright, M. Sprat, J. Wylde, E. H. Carter, and W. H. Routledge.
